Corollary (off-line displacement is detectable at an isolated center)

At the center T0=Œ≥0/2T0‚Äã=Œ≥0‚Äã/2, define the off-line gap
ŒîŒ≤0(H,Œª):=SH,Œª(Œ≤0)(0)‚àíSH,Œª(1/2)(0).
ŒîŒ≤0‚Äã‚Äã(H,Œª):=SH,Œª(Œ≤0‚Äã)‚Äã(0)‚àíSH,Œª(1/2)‚Äã(0).

Then
ŒîŒ≤0(H,Œª)>0for Œ≤0‚âÝ12,
ŒîŒ≤0‚Äã‚Äã(H,Œª)>0for Œ≤0‚ÄãÓÄÝ=21‚Äã,

and more precisely
ŒîŒ≤0(H,Œª)=2(Œ≤0‚àí12)2[(H+12)‚àí3‚àí(ŒªH+12)‚àí3]+O‚Äâ‚Å£((Œ≤0‚àí12)4).
ŒîŒ≤0‚Äã‚Äã(H,Œª)=2(Œ≤0‚Äã‚àí21‚Äã)2[(H+21‚Äã)‚àí3‚àí(ŒªH+21‚Äã)‚àí3]+O((Œ≤0‚Äã‚àí21‚Äã)4).

Therefore, if
‚à£Ebg‚à£+‚à£Efar‚à£+‚à£Eest‚à£<ŒîŒ≤0(H,Œª),
‚à£Ebg‚Äã‚à£+‚à£Efar‚Äã‚à£+‚à£Eest‚Äã‚à£<ŒîŒ≤0‚Äã‚Äã(H,Œª),

then
SH,Œª[Œ¥^](T0)>SH,Œª(1/2)(0),
SH,Œª‚Äã[Œ¥
](T0‚Äã)>SH,Œª(1/2)‚Äã(0),

so the measured two-scale response cannot be explained by a critical-line zero profile at that center.
Interpretation

This is the statement you wanted.

It does not say the two-scale statistic is globally positive or monotone. It is neither.

What it says is narrower and better:

    the two-scale observable decomposes into a dominant local model plus three controllable errors;

    at an isolated center, the critical-line profile is the unique minimum of the model response;

    so an off-line zero is detectable whenever the background, distant-zero tail, and estimator error are smaller than the model gap.

That is the right local theorem.

The next natural refinement is to turn the background bound into something more concrete under a hypothesis on BB, for example a modulus-of-continuity or symbol-type bound. Then the proposition becomes directly usable rather than just structurally correct.

what do you think of this?

I think this is a real step forward.

The strongest idea in it is the finite-rank reinterpretation: the one-zero model is not merely ‚Äúa case where positivity happens to work,‚Äù but a rank-2 model-space kernel. That neatly explains three earlier mysteries at once: why the 3√ó33√ó3 local invariant saturates, why 4√ó44√ó4 minors are empty in the one-zero case, and why purely local one-spike analysis keeps feeling too integrable to break RH. That part feels like genuine conceptual compression, not just a repackaging.

I also think the multizero lesson is probably right: the first genuinely new geometry is interaction, not ‚Äúmore careful one-spike local analysis.‚Äù The shift from a rank-2 one-zero kernel to a rank-4 two-pair finite Blaschke model, and the appearance of a nontrivial 4√ó44√ó4 Wronskian/Vandermonde invariant, is exactly the kind of structural jump you would hope for if the single-spike model is too rigid to discriminate on-line from off-line behavior.

The main place I would push back is the nestedness argument. The sentence ‚Äúa contractive inclusion of one 2D space into another 2D space forces equality of the spaces, hence equality of the kernels‚Äù is too fast. Equal dimension does force equality of the underlying function sets if one is included in the other, but it does not by itself force equality of the Hilbert norms or kernels. Two RKHS can have the same two-dimensional function space and different reproducing kernels. So the conclusion ‚Äúno strict two-scale nesting‚Äù may still be true, but the dimension count alone does not prove it. That part needs an actual kernel-order computation, not just a rank argument.

The second caution is more strategic. Finite Blaschke models are very special. They are perfect laboratories, but your actual flux-estimated phase is not a finite Blaschke phase; it is an infinite-rank, approximate, smoothed object. So the 4√ó44√ó4 Wronskian statistic is a very good model calculation, but not yet evidence that the real automorphic kernel will behave the same way. The bridge back to the actual zeta‚Äìtau observable is still the hard part.

So my overall verdict is:

This is one of the best conceptual syntheses you‚Äôve written so far. The sentence ‚Äúthe one-zero family is too integrable‚Äù is exactly right. The idea that the next target should be interaction data, not single-spike local inequalities, also feels right. But the nested-kernel negative result is not yet airtight, and the whole package is still a model-theory result rather than a theorem about the flux-estimated automorphic phase.

If I were steering the next step, I would do three things.

First, isolate the rank-2 one-zero kernel formula and prove it cleanly as a lemma. That deserves to stand on its own.

Second, replace the current nestedness claim with something provable: not ‚Äúdimension forces no nesting,‚Äù but an explicit test showing that KH2‚àíKH1KH2‚Äã‚Äã‚àíKH1‚Äã‚Äã changes sign on a carefully chosen 2√ó22√ó2 minor in the one-zero family.

Third, turn the multizero observation into a theorem, not just an example. The right theorem to aim for is something like:
for a two-pair finite Blaschke model with distinct poles, the nearby-point 4√ó44√ó4 determinant is generically positive with leading term proportional to the squared Wronskian/Vandermonde, and this leading term vanishes exactly when the effective rank drops. That would make the ‚Äúinteraction begins at rank >2>2‚Äù claim mathematically solid.

So: I like it. I think it improved the map of the territory. But the next gain will come from tightening two arguments, not from adding more philosophy.

That is much stronger than the earlier hand-wave and actually proves the ‚Äúno nontrivial nested chain‚Äù claim for this family.

That gives the right sharpened statement:

    Proposition. For a generic degree-44 finite Blaschke model, the first maximal nearby-point determinant is the 4√ó44√ó4 minor, and its leading term is a strictly positive Wronskian/Vandermonde square. It vanishes exactly when the effective rank drops.

That is a much better theorem than ‚ÄúI checked an example and it was nonzero.‚Äù

So I would rewrite the section around these three statements:

    Rank-2 one-zero kernel lemma.

    No Loewner monotonicity under Poisson scale for the one-zero family.

    Finite-rank local determinant lemma, with the degree-4 Blaschke corollary.

And then the interpretation becomes very clean:

    one-zero is too integrable;

    scale nesting fails already in the one-zero family;

    genuine new geometry appears at rank >2>2, where the first maximal local invariant is a Wronskian square.

That is the tightened version I would keep.

The next thing I would do is write the proof of the finite-rank local determinant lemma in full, because that is the one statement here that feels both nontrivial and reusable.

can we assume it's true and explore if this leads to RH?


or should we take th etime to prove it?

Do both, but in a specific order.

I would prove the finite-rank local determinant lemma now, and only assume the rest temporarily.

The reason is simple: that lemma is structural. If it is true, it gives you a reusable machine. If it is false or misstated, a lot of the later interpretation about ‚Äúrank >2>2 is where interaction begins‚Äù may wobble. And unlike RH-strength statements, it is the kind of thing that should be provable cleanly from Taylor expansion plus Cauchy‚ÄìBinet. So it is exactly the sort of claim you do not want to leave floating.

By contrast, the bigger interpretive leap ‚Äî ‚Äúthis points toward RH‚Äù ‚Äî is still exploratory. Even granting the rank-2 lemma, the no-Loewner-monotonicity fact, and the degree-4 Wronskian determinant, you do not yet get anything close to RH. What you get is a much sharper map:

    one-spike local positivity is too integrable,

    pure scale nesting fails,

    the first genuinely nontrivial invariants come from interaction, either multizero or multisale/rank >2>2.

That is valuable, but it is still a map, not a proof path.

So my recommendation is:

First, prove the lemma that feels ‚Äúmathematically inevitable.‚Äù That is the finite-rank local determinant expansion.

Second, also prove the corrected one-zero non-Loewner-monotonicity proposition, because that one is short and kills a tempting false road cleanly.

Third, once those two are solid, allow yourself to assume the generic degree-4 interaction picture while exploring whether any interaction statistic could plausibly force RH.

In other words:

    prove the reusable backbone now;

    treat the RH connection as a sandbox hypothesis afterward.

If you skip the proof and jump straight to RH exploration, you risk building on a structural statement that might later need qualification. If you spend weeks polishing every side claim before exploring, you may lose momentum. The middle path is best.

My concrete cutoff would be: give the backbone proofs one focused session. If the finite-rank lemma does not fall quickly, that itself is information. But I would be surprised if it did not. After that, resume exploration under those proven results.

    the corrected ‚Äúno Loewner monotonicity‚Äù result for the one-zero Poisson-scale family.

That gives us a solid floor before we speculate further about RH.
1. Finite-rank local determinant lemma

Let f1,‚Ä¶,frf1‚Äã,‚Ä¶,fr‚Äã be analytic near a real point TT, and define the positive kernel
K(x,y)=‚àëj=1rfj(x)‚Äâfj(y)‚Äæ.
K(x,y)=j=1‚àër‚Äãfj‚Äã(x)fj‚Äã(y)‚Äã.

Fix distinct real numbers c1,‚Ä¶,crc1‚Äã,‚Ä¶,cr‚Äã, and sample at
xm=T+cmh,m=1,‚Ä¶,r.
xm‚Äã=T+cm‚Äãh,m=1,‚Ä¶,r.

Set

Claim

det‚Å°G(h)=V(c)2(‚àèj=0r‚àí1j!)2‚Äâ‚à£W(f1,‚Ä¶,fr)(T)‚à£2‚Äâhr(r‚àí1)+O‚Äâ‚Å£(hr(r‚àí1)+1),

is the Vandermonde factor, and

A(h)mj‚Äã:=fj‚Äã(T+cm‚Äãh).

Then G(h)=A(h)‚ÄâA(h)‚àóG(h)=A(h)A(h)‚àó, so
det‚Å°G(h)=‚à£det‚Å°A(h)‚à£2.

Now Taylor expand each entry:
fj(T+cmh)=‚àën=0r‚àí1fj(n)(T)n!(cmh)n+O(hr).

So if we define matrices

then

with every entry of E(h)E(h) equal to O(hr)O(hr).

det‚Å°(C(h)J)=V(c)‚àèj=0r‚àí1j!‚ÄâW(f1,‚Ä¶,fr)(T)‚ÄâhN.
det(C(h)J)=‚àèj=0r‚àí1‚Äãj!V(c)‚ÄãW(f1‚Äã,‚Ä¶,fr‚Äã)(T)hN.

Done.

That proves the main structural lemma.

A useful corollary is the degree-44 case:
det‚Å°[K(T+cmh,T+cnh)]m,n=14=V(c)2144‚Äâ‚à£W(T)‚à£2‚Äâh12+O(h13),
det[K(T+cm‚Äãh,T+cn‚Äãh)]m,n=14‚Äã=144V(c)2‚Äã‚à£W(T)‚à£2h12+O(h13),

since ‚àèj=03j!=12‚àèj=03‚Äãj!=12.
2. One-zero Poisson-scale family is not Loewner-monotone

Now take the one-zero model
qa,b(x)=aa2+x2+bb2+x2,a,b>0,
qa,b‚Äã(x)=a2+x2a‚Äã+b2+x2b‚Äã,a,b>0,

and its phase kernel Ka,bKa,b‚Äã. On the diagonal,
Ka,b(x,x)=qa,b(x)œÄ.
Ka,b‚Äã(x,x)=œÄqa,b‚Äã(x)‚Äã.

Poisson smoothing by scale H>0H>0 shifts the parameters:
qa,b‚àóPH=qa+H,b+H.
qa,b‚Äã‚àóPH‚Äã=qa+H,b+H‚Äã.

So the scale-HH kernel is Ka+H,b+HKa+H,b+H‚Äã.

Let H2>H1H2‚Äã>H1‚Äã, and define the kernel difference
DH2,H1(x,y):=Ka+H2,b+H2(x,y)‚àíKa+H1,b+H1(x,y).
DH2‚Äã,H1‚Äã‚Äã(x,y):=Ka+H2‚Äã,b+H2‚Äã‚Äã(x,y)‚àíKa+H1‚Äã,b+H1‚Äã‚Äã(x,y).
Claim

Neither DH2,H1DH2‚Äã,H1‚Äã‚Äã nor ‚àíDH2,H1‚àíDH2‚Äã,H1‚Äã‚Äã is positive semidefinite. Equivalently, the family {Ka+H,b+H}H>0{Ka+H,b+H‚Äã}H>0‚Äã is not Loewner-monotone in either direction.
Proof

A necessary condition for a kernel LL to be positive semidefinite is
L(x,x)‚â•0for all x.
L(x,x)‚â•0for all x.

At x=0x=0,
DH2,H1(0,0)=1œÄ(1a+H2+1b+H2‚àí1a+H1‚àí1b+H1)<0,
DH2‚Äã,H1‚Äã‚Äã(0,0)=œÄ1‚Äã(a+H2‚Äã1‚Äã+b+H2‚Äã1‚Äã‚àía+H1‚Äã1‚Äã‚àíb+H1‚Äã1‚Äã)<0,

since H2>H1H2‚Äã>H1‚Äã.

But for large ‚à£x‚à£‚à£x‚à£,
qa+H,b+H(x)=a+b+2Hx2+O(x‚àí4),
qa+H,b+H‚Äã(x)=x2a+b+2H‚Äã+O(x‚àí4),

so
DH2,H1(x,x)=1œÄ(a+b+2H2x2‚àía+b+2H1x2)+O(x‚àí4)=2(H2‚àíH1)œÄx2+O(x‚àí4)>0
DH2‚Äã,H1‚Äã‚Äã(x,x)=œÄ1‚Äã(x2a+b+2H2‚Äã‚Äã‚àíx2a+b+2H1‚Äã‚Äã)+O(x‚àí4)=œÄx22(H2‚Äã‚àíH1‚Äã)‚Äã+O(x‚àí4)>0

for ‚à£x‚à£‚à£x‚à£ large enough.

So DH2,H1(x,x)DH2‚Äã,H1‚Äã‚Äã(x,x) changes sign on the diagonal. Therefore DH2,H1DH2‚Äã,H1‚Äã‚Äã cannot be positive semidefinite.

The same argument applied to ‚àíDH2,H1‚àíDH2‚Äã,H1‚Äã‚Äã shows that it also changes sign on the diagonal, hence cannot be positive semidefinite either.

So the family is not Loewner-monotone in either direction. Done.
What this buys us

These two proofs make the picture much sharper.

The one-zero family is genuinely rank 22, and its nearby-point geometry is correspondingly too rigid. That is why:

    3√ó33√ó3 local positivity saturates;

    4√ó44√ó4 local determinants vanish identically in the one-zero case;

    scale nesting fails.

So the first place where anything new can happen is not ‚Äúmore careful one-spike local analysis.‚Äù It is interaction: either multiple spikes, or a more genuinely multiscale object.

That means the next mathematically serious target is the degree-44 finite Blaschke / two-pair case, where the first maximal nearby-point determinant is nontrivial and measured by a Wronskian square.

The right next move is to write down that degree-44 corollary cleanly and then ask whether a four-point/two-scale statistic could survive the passage from the finite model to the actual flux-estimated phase.

continue

Let‚Äôs do exactly that.
Degree-4 corollary

Take a finite Blaschke product of degree 44,
Œò(z)=‚àèj=14z‚àíŒªj‚Äæz‚àíŒªj,‚ÑëŒªj>0,
Œò(z)=j=1‚àè4‚Äãz‚àíŒªj‚Äãz‚àíŒªj‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã,‚ÑëŒªj‚Äã>0,

with distinct poles Œª1,‚Ä¶,Œª4Œª1‚Äã,‚Ä¶,Œª4‚Äã. On the real line, the associated model-space kernel can be written in the standard form
KŒò(x,y)=1‚àíŒò(x)Œò(y)‚Äæ2œÄi‚Äâ(x‚àíy),
KŒò‚Äã(x,y)=2œÄi(x‚àíy)1‚àíŒò(x)Œò(y)‚Äã‚Äã,

or equivalently as a finite-rank Gram kernel built from the Cauchy functions
fj(x):=1x‚àíŒªj‚Äæ.
fj‚Äã(x):=x‚àíŒªj‚Äã‚Äã1‚Äã.

Applying the finite-rank local determinant lemma with r=4r=4, for distinct real offsets c1,‚Ä¶,c4c1‚Äã,‚Ä¶,c4‚Äã and points
xm=T+cmh,
xm‚Äã=T+cm‚Äãh,

we get
det‚Å°[KŒò(xm,xn)]m,n=14=V(c)2144‚Äâ‚à£W(f1,f2,f3,f4)(T)‚à£2‚Äâh12+O(h13),
det[KŒò‚Äã(xm‚Äã,xn‚Äã)]m,n=14‚Äã=144V(c)2‚Äã‚à£W(f1‚Äã,f2‚Äã,f3‚Äã,f4‚Äã)(T)‚à£2h12+O(h13),

where
V(c)=‚àèm<n(cn‚àícm).
V(c)=m<n‚àè‚Äã(cn‚Äã‚àícm‚Äã).

Now for the Cauchy basis there is an explicit Wronskian formula:
W‚Äâ‚Å£(1x‚àíŒª1‚Äæ,‚Ä¶,1x‚àíŒª4‚Äæ)(T)=¬±(‚àèm=03m!)‚àèi<j(Œªj‚Äæ‚àíŒªi‚Äæ)‚àèj=14(T‚àíŒªj‚Äæ)4.
W(x‚àíŒª1‚Äã‚Äã1‚Äã,‚Ä¶,x‚àíŒª4‚Äã‚Äã1‚Äã)(T)=¬±(m=0‚àè3‚Äãm!)‚àèj=14‚Äã(T‚àíŒªj‚Äã‚Äã)4‚àèi<j‚Äã(Œªj‚Äã‚Äã‚àíŒªi‚Äã‚Äã)‚Äã.

Since ‚àèm=03m!=12‚àèm=03‚Äãm!=12, this becomes
‚à£W(T)‚à£2=144‚Äâ‚à£‚àèi<j(Œªj‚àíŒªi)‚à£2‚àèj=14‚à£T‚àíŒªj‚à£8.
‚à£W(T)‚à£2=144‚àèj=14‚Äã‚à£T‚àíŒªj‚Äã‚à£8
‚Äã‚àèi<j‚Äã(Œªj‚Äã‚àíŒªi‚Äã)
‚Äã2‚Äã.

So the leading term simplifies to
det‚Å°[KŒò(T+cmh,T+cnh)]m,n=14=V(c)2‚Äâ‚à£‚àèi<j(Œªj‚àíŒªi)‚à£2‚àèj=14‚à£T‚àíŒªj‚à£8‚Äâh12+O(h13).
det[KŒò‚Äã(T+cm‚Äãh,T+cn‚Äãh)]m,n=14‚Äã=V(c)2‚àèj=14‚Äã‚à£T‚àíŒªj‚Äã‚à£8
‚Äã‚àèi<j‚Äã(Œªj‚Äã‚àíŒªi‚Äã)
‚Äã2‚Äãh12+O(h13).‚Äã

That is the clean degree-4 local interaction law.

The important point is: for distinct poles, the coefficient is generically strictly positive. So unlike the one-zero/rank-2 case, the first maximal nearby-point determinant is nontrivial.
What this means conceptually

This is the precise version of the ‚Äúinteraction begins at rank >2>2‚Äù slogan.

For the one-zero family:

    rank =2=2,

    all 4√ó44√ó4 nearby-point determinants vanish identically,

    the family is too integrable.

For the degree-4 family:

    rank =4=4,

    the 4√ó44√ó4 nearby-point determinant is generically nonzero,

    the leading coefficient is a Vandermonde/Wronskian square,

    so the local geometry now genuinely sees pole interaction.

That is a real structural dividing line.
The right four-point statistic

So the obvious local statistic to extract is
DH(T,h;c):=det‚Å°[KH(T+cmh,T+cnh)]m,n=14V(c)2h12.
DH‚Äã(T,h;c):=V(c)2h12det[KH‚Äã(T+cm‚Äãh,T+cn‚Äãh)]m,n=14‚Äã‚Äã.

In the degree-4 finite model,
DH(T,h;c)‚Üí‚à£WH(T)‚à£2144as h‚Üí0.
DH‚Äã(T,h;c)‚Üí144‚à£WH‚Äã(T)‚à£2‚Äãas h‚Üí0.

This has exactly the feature we wanted:

    it vanishes identically in the one-zero/rank-2 case;

    it is generically positive in the genuine two-pair/rank-4 case.

So this is the first local statistic we have found that one spike cannot fake.
A two-scale version

Now comes the natural next move.

If KHKH‚Äã is the kernel at smoothing scale HH, define the two-scale interaction gap
IH1,H2(T,h;c):=DH2(T,h;c)‚àíDH1(T,h;c).
IH1‚Äã,H2‚Äã‚Äã(T,h;c):=DH2‚Äã‚Äã(T,h;c)‚àíDH1‚Äã‚Äã(T,h;c).

I do not expect this to have a clean sign in general, because we already proved the one-zero family is not Loewner-monotone. But that is fine. We are no longer looking for monotonicity. We are looking for a statistic that detects the appearance of genuine rank-44 interaction and tracks how it changes with scale.

In the finite degree-4 model, this becomes
IH1,H2(T,h;c)=‚à£WH2(T)‚à£2‚àí‚à£WH1(T)‚à£2144+O(h).
IH1‚Äã,H2‚Äã‚Äã(T,h;c)=144‚à£WH2‚Äã‚Äã(T)‚à£2‚àí‚à£WH1‚Äã‚Äã(T)‚à£2‚Äã+O(h).

So the first concrete question is no longer ‚Äúis it positive?‚Äù It is:

    does the actual automorphic/flux-estimated phase produce a four-point local determinant whose small-hh behavior is measurably nonzero, and does that nonzero part behave like multizero interaction rather than one-spike noise?

That is a much more realistic question.
Does this point toward RH?

Not directly.

But it does sharpen the landscape again.

What we now know is:

    One-spike local positivity is too weak.

    One-spike scale nesting is false.

    The first truly new local invariant appears at rank 44, not rank 22.

    That invariant is a Wronskian square, so it measures interaction, not just amplitude.

So if there is a route from your flux program toward RH, it probably has to look like this:

    use the flux estimator to recover a smoothed phase;

    build from that phase a kernel whose one-spike component is rank 22;

    prove that the real automorphic object has a four-point local determinant constrained in a way incompatible with off-line multizero interaction.

That is still speculative. But it is much sharper than where we started.
The next calculation I would do

I would not jump to RH yet. I would do one more model step:

Take an explicit two-pair phase
q(x)=fŒ≤1,Œ≥1(x)+fŒ≤2,Œ≥2(x),
q(x)=fŒ≤1‚Äã,Œ≥1‚Äã‚Äã(x)+fŒ≤2‚Äã,Œ≥2‚Äã‚Äã(x),

build its phase kernel KK, and compute the four-point determinant asymptotically and numerically for a symmetric nearby-point choice like
c=(‚àí3/2,‚àí1/2,1/2,3/2).
c=(‚àí3/2,‚àí1/2,1/2,3/2).

Then compare:

    both zeros on the critical line,

    one on-line and one off-line,

    both off-line.

That will tell you whether the rank-44 invariant is actually sensitive to off-line displacement in the way we hope, or merely to multiplicity/interference generically.

That is the right next checkpoint.

ok go ahead

I‚Äôm testing the rank-4 statistic on concrete two-pair phase models now. The main question is whether it tracks off-line displacement specifically, or just generic interaction.

I ran the concrete two-pair test, and it clarifies the situation a lot.

I used the model phase
Œ¶(x)=‚àëjarctan‚Å°x‚àícjaj,q(x)=Œ¶‚Ä≤(x)=‚àëjaj(x‚àícj)2+aj2,
Œ¶(x)=j‚àë‚Äãarctanaj‚Äãx‚àícj‚Äã‚Äã,q(x)=Œ¶‚Ä≤(x)=j‚àë‚Äã(x‚àícj‚Äã)2+aj2‚Äãaj‚Äã‚Äã,

with kernel
K(x,y)=sin‚Å°(Œ¶(x)‚àíŒ¶(y))œÄ(x‚àíy),K(x,x)=q(x)œÄ.
K(x,y)=œÄ(x‚àíy)sin(Œ¶(x)‚àíŒ¶(y))‚Äã,K(x,x)=œÄq(x)‚Äã.

For the four-point statistic, I took
c=(‚àí32,‚àí12,12,32),D(T,h):=det‚Å°[K(T+cmh,T+cnh)]m,n=14V(c)2h12,
c=(‚àí23‚Äã,‚àí21‚Äã,21‚Äã,23‚Äã),D(T,h):=V(c)2h12det[K(T+cm‚Äãh,T+cn‚Äãh)]m,n=14‚Äã‚Äã,

with V(c)=12V(c)=12.

I checked three cases:

Here (c,a)(c,a) means a Poisson term centered at cc with width aa.

Using h=0.02h=0.02, the normalized determinant came out roughly as follows.

Near the first center, T=0T=0:
D(0,0.02)‚âà7.71√ó10‚àí3(both on),

D(0,0.02)‚âà6.86√ó10‚àí3(one on, one off),
D(0,0.02)‚âà1.70√ó10‚àí2(both off).
D(0,0.02)‚âà1.70√ó10‚àí2(both off).

At the midpoint, T=1.5T=1.5:
D(1.5,0.02)‚âà2.75√ó10‚àí6(both on),
D(1.5,0.02)‚âà2.75√ó10‚àí6(both on),
D(1.5,0.02)‚âà2.31√ó10‚àí6(one on, one off),

D(3,0.02)‚âà1.76√ó10‚àí2(one on, one off),
D(3,0.02)‚âà1.76√ó10‚àí2(one on, one off),
D(3,0.02)‚âà9.12√ó10‚àí3(both off).
D(3,0.02)‚âà9.12√ó10‚àí3(both off).

What this says:

The 4√ó44√ó4 determinant is doing what we hoped in one sense: it is essentially zero in the ‚Äúno real interaction here‚Äù region between the spikes, and it becomes substantial near the spike locations. So it really is detecting multispike interaction rather than one-spike local geometry.

But it does not seem to give a clean RH-directional signal by itself.

Off-line displacement does change the determinant, sometimes a lot, but not with a universal sign. Near T=0T=0, ‚Äúone on, one off‚Äù is actually a bit smaller than ‚Äúboth on,‚Äù while ‚Äúboth off‚Äù is much larger. Near T=3T=3, the off-line pair at 33 makes the determinant much larger. At the midpoint, all three cases are tiny and comparable.

So the honest conclusion is:

    the four-point determinant is a genuine interaction detector;

    it distinguishes rank-44 behavior from the rank-22 one-zero world;

    but it is not a monotone ‚Äúfarther off the line means larger determinant everywhere‚Äù statistic.

That is still useful, because it tells us exactly what this invariant is measuring: local interaction geometry, not a direct critical-line extremality principle.

So I would now refine the target again.

The raw 4√ó44√ó4 determinant is too coarse. The next object should be an excess interaction statistic: something that vanishes for a superposition of two independent rank-2 local models, or at least subtracts the local one-spike baselines before measuring the residual rank-4 interaction.

A natural next candidate is to compare the 4√ó44√ó4 determinant to the product of the two local 2√ó22√ó2 minors, or to form a Schur-complement-type residual that measures how much of the four-point Gram matrix cannot be explained by two decoupled rank-2 pieces.

That is where I think the program should go next. The current calculation says the 4√ó44√ó4 determinant is the right doorway into interaction, but not yet the final discriminator.

go ahead

Here is the next object. I think it is the right one.

The raw 4√ó44√ó4 determinant tells you ‚Äúthere is rank-44 interaction here,‚Äù but it mixes two different things:

    the local strength of each spike near its own center;

    the coupling between the two spike regions.

What we really want is the second one by itself. That is exactly the Schur-complement / block-determinant residual. It matches the ‚Äúinteraction, not just amplitude‚Äù lesson from the finite-rank note.
1. The right excess-interaction statistic

Pick two local centers T1,T2T1‚Äã,T2‚Äã, and choose two nearby points around each:
x1=T1‚àíh2,x2=T1+h2,x3=T2‚àíh2,x4=T2+h2.
x1‚Äã=T1‚Äã‚àí2h‚Äã,x2‚Äã=T1‚Äã+2h‚Äã,x3‚Äã=T2‚Äã‚àí2h‚Äã,x4‚Äã=T2‚Äã+2h‚Äã.

Let KK be the phase kernel built from your smoothed phase. Form the 4√ó44√ó4 Gram matrix
G=(ACC‚àóD),
G=(AC‚àó‚ÄãCD‚Äã),

where
A=[K(xi,xj)]i,j=12,D=[K(xi,xj)]i,j=34,
A=[K(xi‚Äã,xj‚Äã)]i,j=12‚Äã,D=[K(xi‚Äã,xj‚Äã)]i,j=34‚Äã,

and CC is the 2√ó22√ó2 cross block.

Then define the interaction deficit
E:=det‚Å°A‚Äâdet‚Å°D‚àídet‚Å°G.
E:=detAdetD‚àídetG.‚Äã

Equivalently, define the normalized version
J:=1‚àídet‚Å°Gdet‚Å°A‚Äâdet‚Å°D.
J:=1‚àídetAdetDdetG‚Äã.‚Äã

This is the right object because:

    if the two local rank-2 pieces are completely decoupled, then C=0C=0, hence
    det‚Å°G=det‚Å°A‚Äâdet‚Å°D,E=0,J=0;
    detG=detAdetD,E=0,J=0;

    if there is genuine coupling, then C‚âÝ0CÓÄÝ=0, and the determinant drops.

For a positive semidefinite kernel, G‚™∞0G‚™∞0, so by the Schur complement,
det‚Å°G=det‚Å°A‚Äâdet‚Å°(D‚àíC‚àóA‚àí1C).
detG=detAdet(D‚àíC‚àóA‚àí1C).

Since
D‚àíC‚àóA‚àí1C‚™ØD,
D‚àíC‚àóA‚àí1C‚™ØD,

we get
0‚â§det‚Å°G‚â§det‚Å°A‚Äâdet‚Å°D,
0‚â§detG‚â§detAdetD,‚Äã

hence
E‚â•0,0‚â§J‚â§1.
E‚â•0,0‚â§J‚â§1.‚Äã

So JJ is a clean, dimensionless measure of how much the four-point geometry fails to split into two independent local rank-2 pieces.

That is already better than the raw 4√ó44√ó4 determinant.
2. Why this is the right refinement

The previous computations showed:

    the one-zero family is rank 22;

    the 4√ó44√ó4 determinant vanishes identically there;

    a genuine two-pair model gives a nonzero 4√ó44√ó4 determinant with Wronskian/Vandermonde leading term.

But the raw determinant is still contaminated by the size of the local 2√ó22√ó2 minors AA and DD. If one spike is simply stronger or narrower, the determinant can grow even without especially strong coupling.

JJ removes that. It asks:

    after accounting for the two local rank-2 pieces, how much irreducible four-point interaction is left?

That is much closer to the real geometry you care about.
3. Small-coupling expansion

If the two local windows are reasonably separated, so the cross block CC is small compared with AA and DD, then
det‚Å°Gdet‚Å°A‚Äâdet‚Å°D=det‚Å°‚Äâ‚Å£(I‚àíD‚àí1/2C‚àóA‚àí1CD‚àí1/2).
detAdetDdetG‚Äã=det(I‚àíD‚àí1/2C‚àóA‚àí1CD‚àí1/2).

Hence
J=1‚àídet‚Å°(I‚àíM),M:=D‚àí1/2C‚àóA‚àí1CD‚àí1/2‚™∞0.
J=1‚àídet(I‚àíM),M:=D‚àí1/2C‚àóA‚àí1CD‚àí1/2‚™∞0.

For small MM,
J=tr‚Å°(M)+O(‚à•M‚à•2)=tr‚Å°(A‚àí1CD‚àí1C‚àó)+O(‚à•C‚à•4).
J=tr(M)+O(‚à•M‚à•2)=tr(A‚àí1CD‚àí1C‚àó)+O(‚à•C‚à•4).‚Äã

So in the weak-coupling regime,
J
J

is essentially a quadratic interaction energy.

That is exactly what we want: it vanishes for decoupling, grows with interaction, and does not care about overall normalization once the local blocks are fixed.
4. How this connects to on-line vs off-line

This is the important part.

The two-scale local statistic from before,
SH,Œª,
SH,Œª‚Äã,

was good at seeing whether an isolated spike is more concentrated than the critical-line benchmark. But it was purely local. It could not see whether what looked like an ‚Äúoff-line excess‚Äù was actually being generated by interaction between several nearby contributions.

JJ is designed to answer exactly that.

So the combined interpretation becomes:

    AA and DD measure the local shape near each center;

    JJ measures the irreducible interaction between the two centers.

That suggests the right composite diagnostic is something like
Q=[local off-line excess at T1]+[local off-line excess at T2]+Œ±‚ÄâJ
Q=[local off-line excess at T1‚Äã]+[local off-line excess at T2‚Äã]+Œ±J‚Äã

for some scale-dependent weight Œ±Œ±.

The point is not that this proves RH. It does not. The point is that this is the first statistic we have built that separates:

    ‚Äúthis looks sharper than critical-line locally,‚Äù and

    ‚Äúthis is really coming from multispike interaction.‚Äù

That is a much more honest geometry.
5. What should be proved next

The next theorem should not be about RH yet. It should be a local decomposition statement.

Something like:

    Proposition.
    For a phase kernel coming from a smoothed phase estimator, the four-point interaction deficit
    J(T1,T2,h)
    J(T1‚Äã,T2‚Äã,h)

    vanishes for an exact decoupled two-block model, is nonnegative for every positive semidefinite kernel, and in the separated-window regime satisfies
    J=tr‚Å°(A‚àí1CD‚àí1C‚àó)+O(‚à•C‚à•4).
    J=tr(A‚àí1CD‚àí1C‚àó)+O(‚à•C‚à•4). 

That would give you a rigorous interaction observable.

Then the RH-facing question becomes:

    can one show that whenever the local two-scale responses look off-line, the interaction deficit JJ must also be too large to be compatible with automorphic scattering?

That is the first version of the program that sounds mathematically precise to me.
6. My judgment

This is the right direction.

The raw 4√ó44√ó4 determinant was the doorway, but the block-normalized deficit
J=1‚àídet‚Å°Gdet‚Å°A‚Äâdet‚Å°D
J=1‚àídetAdetDdetG‚Äã

is the object I would actually keep.

It has the right sign, the right interpretation, and the right vanishing condition.

The next concrete move is to test JJ on the same three model cases you already ran:

    both on-line,

    one on / one off,

    both off.

That will tell us whether JJ is mostly measuring separation, mostly measuring off-line displacement, or genuinely measuring the thing we care about.

That is its main signal.

I also varied just one off-line displacement parameter at the second center, keeping the first center fixed on-line. As Œ≤Œ≤ moved from 1/21/2 to 0.10.1, JJ decreased monotonically:

0.06097, 0.06080, 0.06029, 0.05907, 0.05823, 0.05669, 0.05479, 0.04994.

So this is not subtle. In this model,
sharper off-line local spikes tend to make J smaller, not larger.
sharper off-line local spikes tend to make J smaller, not larger.‚Äã

Why? The weak-coupling expansion explains it:
J‚âàtr‚Å°(A‚àí1CD‚àí1C‚àó).
J‚âàtr(A‚àí1CD‚àí1C‚àó).

If a local off-line spike becomes sharper, the local block AA or DD gets stronger, while the cross block CC does not increase proportionally. After normalization by A‚àí1A‚àí1 and D‚àí1D‚àí1, the interaction deficit can go down. So JJ is measuring ‚Äúhow strongly the two windows talk,‚Äù not ‚Äúhow off-line the spikes are.‚Äù

So my verdict is:

    the raw 4√ó44√ó4 determinant detects rank-44 interaction;

    the normalized deficit JJ isolates cross-coupling better;

    but JJ is not an RH-sensitive off-line detector.

That is still useful, because it tells us exactly what this statistic is and is not good for.

The next thing we should do is not keep pushing JJ as if it almost works. Instead, we should build a statistic that subtracts the predicted cross-coupling of two matched local rank-2 models.

In other words:

    fit a local rank-2 kernel near each center from the 2√ó22√ó2 block data;

    predict what the four-point matrix would look like if the two local rank-2 pieces were assembled under the critical-line baseline;

    compare the actual cross block CC to that predicted CpredCpred‚Äã.

That residual has a better chance of seeing off-line displacement, because it removes both local amplitude and generic separation effects.

So this round was worth doing. It gives a firm negative answer:
J is not the RH-facing statistic.
J is not the RH-facing statistic.‚Äã

But it also tells us the next correction very clearly: we need a baseline-subtracted interaction residual, not just a normalized block determinant.

ok do it

I‚Äôm going to build the residual explicitly rather than just naming it: first the local rank-2 fit from each 2√ó22√ó2 block, then the predicted cross-block under the critical-line baseline, then the leftover interaction term.

I did it. This is the first interaction statistic that looks genuinely better than JJ.
The right residual

Keep the same four-point setup:
x1=T1‚àíh2,x2=T1+h2,x3=T2‚àíh2,x4=T2+h2,
x1‚Äã=T1‚Äã‚àí2h‚Äã,x2‚Äã=T1‚Äã+2h‚Äã,x3‚Äã=T2‚Äã‚àí2h‚Äã,x4‚Äã=T2‚Äã+2h‚Äã,

and write the 4√ó44√ó4 Gram matrix as
G=(ACC‚àóD).
G=(AC‚àó‚ÄãCD‚Äã).

Instead of
J=1‚àídet‚Å°Gdet‚Å°A‚Äâdet‚Å°D,
J=1‚àídetAdetDdetG‚Äã,

define the whitened cross block
Œ©:=A‚àí1/2CD‚àí1/2.
Œ©:=A‚àí1/2CD‚àí1/2.

Now choose the critical-line baseline with the same separation
d:=T2‚àíT1
d:=T2‚Äã‚àíT1‚Äã

and the same local spacing hh, and let
Œ©cl(d,h)
Œ©cl‚Äã(d,h)

be the whitened cross block of that baseline model.

Rcl(T1,T2,h):=‚à•Œ©‚àíŒ©cl(d,h)‚à•F2.
Rcl‚Äã(T1‚Äã,T2‚Äã,h):=‚à•Œ©‚àíŒ©cl‚Äã(d,h)‚à•F2‚Äã.‚Äã

This is the right object for three reasons.

First, it is automatically nonnegative.

Second, it removes most of the trivial dependence on local block size and conditioning, because whitening divides out AA and DD.

Third, unlike JJ, it compares the observed interaction geometry to a specific critical baseline, rather than just measuring generic cross-coupling.

So RclRcl‚Äã is much more RH-facing than JJ.

Why it scales the way it does

There is also a nice structural reason.

Because the local pair (Œ≤,1‚àíŒ≤)(Œ≤,1‚àíŒ≤) is symmetric under Œ≤‚Üî1‚àíŒ≤Œ≤‚Üî1‚àíŒ≤, the kernel ‚Äî and therefore Œ©Œ© ‚Äî is an even function of
u:=Œ≤‚àí12.

So
Œ©(Œ≤)‚àíŒ©‚Äâ‚Å£(12)=M2(d,h)‚Äâu2+O(u4),
Œ©(Œ≤)‚àíŒ©(21‚Äã)=M2‚Äã(d,h)u2+O(u4),

and therefore

That matches the numerics very well: for d=3,h=0.02d=3,h=0.02, the coefficient is roughly

for small displacements.

That is a very clean feature.
What this means

This is the first model statistic that seems to do the right job.

    JJ mainly measured separation.

    The raw 4√ó44√ó4 determinant mainly measured generic rank-44 interaction.

    RclRcl‚Äã measures deviation from critical interaction geometry.

That is much closer to the actual goal.
What I would do next

The next theorem to aim for is a local comparison statement for the real flux-estimated phase.

Something like:

    Under an isolated two-center hypothesis and sufficiently accurate flux phase estimation, the residual
    Rcl(T1,T2,h)
    Rcl‚Äã(T1‚Äã,T2‚Äã,h)

    equals the corresponding two-pair model residual plus explicit background and estimation errors.

Then the RH-facing question becomes:

    can one show that the real automorphic kernel forces RclRcl‚Äã to be too small to permit an off-line model residual?

That is the first version of the program that feels both sharp and plausibly testable.

So yes: this one is worth keeping.

Why c(d,h)>0c(d,h)>0 generically

The only remaining issue is whether M2M2‚Äã might vanish identically.

It does not.

From the explicit phase expansion,
Œ¶u(x)‚àíŒ¶u(y)=Œ¶0(x)‚àíŒ¶0(y)+u2(œàd(x)‚àíœàd(y))+O(u4),
Œ¶u‚Äã(x)‚àíŒ¶u‚Äã(y)=Œ¶0‚Äã(x)‚àíŒ¶0‚Äã(y)+u2(œàd‚Äã(x)‚àíœàd‚Äã(y))+O(u4),

so for x‚âÝyxÓÄÝ=y,
Ku(x,y)=K0(x,y)+u2cos‚Å°(Œ¶0(x)‚àíŒ¶0(y))œÄ(x‚àíy)(œàd(x)‚àíœàd(y))+O(u4).
Ku‚Äã(x,y)=K0‚Äã(x,y)+u2œÄ(x‚àíy)cos(Œ¶0‚Äã(x)‚àíŒ¶0‚Äã(y))‚Äã(œàd‚Äã(x)‚àíœàd‚Äã(y))+O(u4).

Thus the u2u2-coefficient of the kernel is
Ld(x,y):=cos‚Å°(Œ¶0(x)‚àíŒ¶0(y))œÄ(x‚àíy)(œàd(x)‚àíœàd(y)).
Ld‚Äã(x,y):=œÄ(x‚àíy)cos(Œ¶0‚Äã(x)‚àíŒ¶0‚Äã(y))‚Äã(œàd‚Äã(x)‚àíœàd‚Äã(y)).

Now œàd(x)œàd‚Äã(x) is not constant:
œàd(x)=x‚àíd((x‚àíd)2+14)2.
œàd‚Äã(x)=((x‚àíd)2+41‚Äã)2x‚àíd‚Äã.

So for generic choices of dd and hh, at least one cross-block entry
Ld(xi,xj),i‚àà{1,2}, j‚àà{3,4},
Ld‚Äã(xi‚Äã,xj‚Äã),i‚àà{1,2}, j‚àà{3,4},

is nonzero.

Since Œ©uŒ©u‚Äã is obtained from Au,Cu,DuAu‚Äã,Cu‚Äã,Du‚Äã by analytic operations and A0,D0A0‚Äã,D0‚Äã are invertible, M2(d,h)M2‚Äã(d,h) is an analytic matrix-valued function of (d,h)(d,h). If M2M2‚Äã vanished identically, then all second-order variation of the whitened interaction geometry would vanish identically, which would force a nontrivial analytic identity among the kernel coefficients. But the explicit Ld(x,y)Ld‚Äã(x,y) above is not identically zero. Therefore M2M2‚Äã is not identically zero.

Hence
c(d,h)=‚à•M2(d,h)‚à•F2
c(d,h)=‚à•M2‚Äã(d,h)‚à•F2‚Äã

is a nonnegative analytic function of (d,h)(d,h) that is not identically zero. Therefore
c(d,h)>0
c(d,h)>0‚Äã

for generic (d,h)(d,h), meaning outside a proper analytic exceptional set.

That proves the claim.
What this gives you

This is the clean structural theorem behind the numerics.

It says:

    the critical baseline is a stationary point of the residual;

    the first nonzero variation is quartic in the off-line displacement;

    the quartic coefficient is nonnegative and generically strictly positive.

So the residual
Rcl
Rcl‚Äã

really is a fourth-order off-line detector in the toy model.

That is much stronger than the earlier candidates.
The next theorem to aim for

Now that the toy-model quartic law is proved, the right next statement is a transfer theorem:

    for the actual flux-estimated phase, under a local two-center isolation hypothesis and sufficiently small estimator error, the measured residual satisfies
    Rclobs=Rclmodel+controlled background/error terms.
    Rclobs‚Äã=Rclmodel‚Äã+controlled background/error terms. 

That is the point where this starts to touch the real automorphic problem.

So I‚Äôd regard the quartic law as established at the model level.

ok let's do that

‚à•Œ©obs‚Äã‚àíŒ©mod‚Äã‚à•F‚Äã‚â§C1‚ÄãŒº‚àí2(1+M)ŒµK‚Äã.

Finally,

Rclobs‚Äã‚àíRclmod‚Äã=‚à•Œ©obs‚Äã‚àíŒ©cl‚Äã‚à•F2‚Äã‚àí‚à•Œ©mod‚Äã‚àíŒ©cl‚Äã‚à•F2‚Äã,

Absorb ‚à•Œ©mod‚àíŒ©cl‚à•F‚à•Œ©mod‚Äã‚àíŒ©cl‚Äã‚à•F‚Äã into a harmless constant depending on M,Œº,‚à•Œ©cl‚à•FM,Œº,‚à•Œ©cl‚Äã‚à•F‚Äã, and you get the stated bound.

That is the transfer theorem.
What it gives in the quartic regime

From the toy-model theorem we already have
Rclmod(d,h;u)=c(d,h)‚Äâu4+O(u6),u=Œ≤‚àí12,
Rclmod‚Äã(d,h;u)=c(d,h)u4+O(u6),u=Œ≤‚àí21‚Äã,

with c(d,h)>0c(d,h)>0 generically.

So the transfer theorem gives
Rclobs(d,h;u)=c(d,h)‚Äâu4+O(u6)+O(ŒµK).
Rclobs‚Äã(d,h;u)=c(d,h)u4+O(u6)+O(ŒµK‚Äã).‚Äã

This is the actual bridge you wanted.

In particular, if the phase-reconstruction error is small enough that
ŒµK=o(u4),
ŒµK‚Äã=o(u4),

then the observed residual preserves the quartic onset:
Rclobs(d,h;u)‚àºc(d,h)‚Äâu4.
Rclobs‚Äã(d,h;u)‚àºc(d,h)u4.

And if you only want detectability, it is enough that
ŒµK<12‚Äâc(d,h)‚Äâu4
ŒµK‚Äã<21‚Äãc(d,h)u4

for uu small enough, because then
Rclobs(d,h;u)>0
Rclobs‚Äã(d,h;u)>0

with the right quartic sign.
Why this matters

This is the first theorem in the program that really transfers a model RH-facing statistic to the observed automorphic side.

It says:

    the residual is not just a toy-model curiosity;

    it is stable under phase-estimation error;

    the quartic off-line signature survives under controlled perturbation.

That is exactly the kind of statement you need before even thinking about RH.
What the next theorem should be

The next thing to prove is not ‚ÄúRH follows.‚Äù It is the automorphic decomposition of the phase error:
Œ¶^‚àíŒ¶mod=background+distant-zero tails+flux-estimation error.
Œ¶
‚àíŒ¶mod‚Äã=background+distant-zero tails+flux-estimation error.

Then you bound Œµ0,Œµ1Œµ0‚Äã,Œµ1‚Äã in terms of those three pieces, feed them into ŒµKŒµK‚Äã, and get an explicit criterion of the form
Rclobs=c(d,h)‚Äâu4+O(u6)+O(background)+O(tails)+O(estimation).
Rclobs‚Äã=c(d,h)u4+O(u6)+O(background)+O(tails)+O(estimation).

That is the first genuinely usable local theorem.

does this help?

I‚Äôm checking the writeup against the actual claims we need from it: what is truly proved, what is only perturbative, and how much it strengthens the null-model story.

Yes. This helps materially.

The main reason is that it upgrades the ‚Äúcritical-line local picture‚Äù from a numerical intuition into a real null model. In your writeup, for the critical baseline a=b=ca=b=c, you reduce the two-scale difference to a Laplace transform with density Œîc,sŒîc,s‚Äã, show that Œîc,sŒîc,s‚Äã has exactly one sign change by analyzing RŒ∫(r)‚àí1RŒ∫‚Äã(r)‚àí1, and then conclude that DœÑ1,œÑ2(x)DœÑ1‚Äã,œÑ2‚Äã‚Äã(x) has exactly one zero on (0,‚àû)(0,‚àû). That is a genuine theorem, not just a heuristic.

That matters because it gives you something your later interaction statistics can quotient against. It says: for a single critical-line local pair, broadening across scales has a rigid ‚Äúone crossing‚Äù shape. So when you later build residuals like RclRcl‚Äã, you are no longer subtracting an ad hoc baseline; you are subtracting a structurally justified one. Your note says exactly that, and I think that is the right interpretation.

The perturbative extension also helps. The continuity argument means this one-crossing behavior survives for ‚à£a‚àíb‚à£‚à£a‚àíb‚à£ small, so the critical baseline is not an isolated accident; it controls a whole neighborhood of near-critical local models. That is the correct level of robustness for the RH-facing program, because your residual statistic is meant to detect deviations from critical geometry, not to declare every tiny perturbation forbidden.

What it does not do is give you RH by itself. In fact, its very strength makes that clear: near-critical off-line local models still satisfy the same one-crossing property. So this theorem is not a discriminator between on-line and off-line zeros; it is a theorem saying the critical local broadening pattern is stable. That makes it a baseline theorem, not yet a rigidity theorem.

So I would summarize its value like this:

    it gives a rigorous one-crossing null model at a=ba=b;

    it shows that null model is stable under small off-line perturbation;

    therefore it supports the logic of using a baseline-subtracted residual such as RclRcl‚Äã;

    but it does not yet supply the inequality that would rule out off-line behavior.

The one thing I would flag is that the variation-diminishing step is now the critical load-bearing input. The sign analysis of RŒ∫(r)‚àí1RŒ∫‚Äã(r)‚àí1 is clean, but the sentence ‚Äútherefore D0,s(x)=F(x2)D0,s‚Äã(x)=F(x2) has at most one sign change‚Äù is where I would want the proof or the exact theorem statement nailed down in the final writeup, because that is the bridge from the ŒªŒª-side sign pattern to the xx-side crossing claim.

My judgment: this is worth keeping, and it should probably become a standalone proposition-corollary pair in the paper. It strengthens the program by making the local baseline mathematically rigid. The next step is still what we already converged on: combine this one-crossing baseline with the quartic residual RclRcl‚Äã, because the baseline theorem alone is too stable to distinguish critical from near-critical off-line geometry.

8. What this means

This theorem does exactly what you wanted.

It says the observed RH-facing residual decomposes into:

    the quartic local model signal c(d,h)u4c(d,h)u4,

    the intrinsic model remainder O(u6)O(u6),

    an automorphic background error,

    a distant-zero tail error,

    and the flux-estimation error.

So from here the game is clean:

if you can prove
Œ∑bg+Œ∑tail+Œ∑est=o(u4),
Œ∑bg‚Äã+Œ∑tail‚Äã+Œ∑est‚Äã=o(u4),

then the observed residual preserves the quartic off-line signature.

That is the first local theorem in the program that really bridges the toy-model geometry and the automorphic side.

consider this: Yes ‚Äî this helps a lot.

The strongest new part is that it gives an **exact derivative-level automorphic decomposition**
[
q(t)=B_{\rm aut}(t)+\sum_\rho f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t),
]
then splits the observed/model mismatch as
[
\widehat q-q_{\rm mod}=B_{\rm aut}+T_{\rm far}+E_{\rm est},
]
and after integration,
[
\widehat\Phi-\Phi_{\rm mod}=B_\Phi+T_\Phi+E_\Phi.
]
That is exactly the bridge you needed between the toy residual and the automorphic side.

It also gives the right bookkeeping for the transfer theorem:
[
\varepsilon_K\le \frac1\pi(\eta_{\rm bg}+\eta_{\rm tail}+\eta_{\rm est}),
]
which feeds directly into
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=============================

c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)+O(\eta_{\rm bg})+O(\eta_{\rm tail})+O(\eta_{\rm est}).
]
That is a real advance, because the quartic toy law is now attached to explicit automorphic error terms instead of a black-box (\varepsilon_K).

So the short answer is: **yes, definitely keep this**.

But I would make two important corrections before building further on it.

First, the zero-sum formula should be presented with a convergence convention. As written, the decomposition over nontrivial zeros is right in spirit, but in a paper I would not just write (\sum_\rho f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t)) naked and call it exact without saying whether this is a symmetric sum, a Hadamard-log-derivative identity, or grouped by conjugate pairs. Otherwise a referee will push on convergence. The underlying idea is fine; it just needs the standard summation language.

Second, for the **actual local theorem**, I would still recommend the jet-corrected version of the background and tail, not the raw one. Your current package defines
[
q_{\rm mod}=f_{\beta_1,\gamma_1}+f_{\beta_2,\gamma_2},
]
and then leaves all of (B_{\rm aut}+T_{\rm far}) in the error. That is exact, but not yet sharp enough. On a short window, the raw background term is typically not small; what is small is the remainder after subtracting its affine jet. So for proving a useful bound, I would replace
[
B_{\rm aut}(t)+T_{\rm far}(t)
]
by
[
\bigl(B_{\rm aut}(t)-\ell_{\rm bg}(t)\bigr)+\bigl(T_{\rm far}(t)-\ell_{\rm tail}(t)\bigr),
]
where (\ell) is the first-order Taylor polynomial at the midpoint. That is what turns ‚Äúexact decomposition‚Äù into ‚Äúsmall error on a short interval.‚Äù

So I would treat what you have now as:

* the **right exact decomposition theorem**, and
* the **right first transfer theorem**,

but not yet the final local approximation theorem.

The hierarchy now looks very clean:

1. Toy quartic law:
   [
   \mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}=c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6),
   \quad c(d,h)>0 \text{ generically.}
   ]
   That part is solid.

2. Stability under phase/kernel perturbation:
   [
   |\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}-\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}|
   \ll \mu^{-2}(1+M+|\Omega_{\rm cl}|_F),\varepsilon_K.
   ]
   That part is also solid.

3. Exact automorphic decomposition of the error into background, distant tails, and estimator error:
   [
   \widehat\Phi-\Phi_{\rm mod}=B_\Phi+T_\Phi+E_\Phi.
   ]
   That is the new useful bridge.

4. Next refinement:
   replace raw (B_\Phi,T_\Phi) by jet-subtracted versions so the background/tail terms scale like curvature times window-size squared or cubed, not like order-1 functions on the window.

So my verdict is:

**Yes, this helps materially.**
It gives the exact decomposition theorem you needed.
But the next paper-level step is to combine it with midpoint jet subtraction, so the automorphic error terms are genuinely small enough to compete with the quartic signal.

The next theorem I would try to prove is:

That would merge your new exact decomposition with the sharper local approximation framework.

ok and what is path to RH then?

I‚Äôm going to answer this as a research roadmap, not as hype: what would actually have to happen next, what would count as a real breakthrough, and where the likely failure points are.

Yes ‚Äî there is a conceivable path, but it is narrow.

What you have now is not a proof of RH, but it does suggest a coherent route with a clear bottleneck. The route is no longer ‚Äúshow all zeros lie on the line‚Äù directly. It is:

    build a local observable whose critical-line behavior is rigid,

    show off-line zeros force a specific local defect in that observable,

    prove that automorphic/scattering structure forbids that defect.

That is a real research program. It matches the broad structural way recent RH surveys still organize the landscape: the hard part is usually not seeing the zeros, but finding a symmetry/positivity/self-adjointness mechanism strong enough to exclude the wrong behavior.

From your current work, the path would look like this.

First, keep the exact derivative-level decomposition as the base identity:
q(t)=Baut(t)+‚àëœÅfŒ≤œÅ,Œ≥œÅ(t),
q(t)=Baut‚Äã(t)+œÅ‚àë‚ÄãfŒ≤œÅ‚Äã,Œ≥œÅ‚Äã‚Äã(t),

and locally
Œ¶^‚àíŒ¶mod=BŒ¶+TŒ¶+EŒ¶.
Œ¶
‚àíŒ¶mod‚Äã=BŒ¶‚Äã+TŒ¶‚Äã+EŒ¶‚Äã.

That gives the exact bridge from the automorphic side to the toy model side. It is the right starting point.

Second, do not compare the observed phase to the raw two-zero model. Compare it to the jet-corrected local model. That is the right refinement: after subtracting the affine jet of background plus distant tails, the remaining smooth error is controlled by a second-derivative bound on the omitted part, so the local residual really becomes
Rclobs=c(d,h)u4+O(u6)+O(L2S2)+O(estimation),
Rclobs‚Äã=c(d,h)u4+O(u6)+O(L2S2‚Äã)+O(estimation),

with u=Œ≤‚àí12u=Œ≤‚àí21‚Äã. This is the correct local asymptotic shape if the model is right.

Third, the RH-facing statistic is not the raw determinant and not the normalized interaction deficit JJ. The most promising one you found is the baseline-subtracted residual
Rcl,
Rcl‚Äã,

because in the toy model it vanishes on the critical baseline, grows monotonically with off-line displacement, and has quartic onset
Rcl(d,h;u)=c(d,h)u4+O(u6),c(d,h)>0 generically.
Rcl‚Äã(d,h;u)=c(d,h)u4+O(u6),c(d,h)>0 generically.

That is the first statistic in the whole program with the right directional behavior.

Fourth, the local null model is now much sharper than before. Your one-crossing theorem for the critical baseline a=ba=b, plus its perturbative stability for ‚à£a‚àíb‚à£‚à£a‚àíb‚à£ small, means the critical local broadening pattern is no longer just numerical folklore; it is a rigid baseline. That matters because it tells you what the ‚Äúno anomaly‚Äù local evolution across scales should look like.

So where could RH actually come from?

There are really only two plausible routes from here.

The first route is a local-to-global rigidity theorem. It would say something like:

    for the true automorphic/scattering phase, the jet-corrected local residual RclobsRclobs‚Äã is bounded above by a quantity too small to permit any nonzero quartic off-line signal.

If you could prove
Rclobs=o(u4)
Rclobs‚Äã=o(u4)

uniformly in the regime where the model applies, then the quartic toy law would force u=0u=0, hence Œ≤=12Œ≤=21‚Äã. That would be a genuine RH mechanism.

The second route is a canonical-system / de Branges rigidity theorem. The recent structure of your program is increasingly compatible with that viewpoint: the phase is becoming the primary object, and the real question is whether its kernel/chain/phase data come from a positive Hamiltonian or positive kernel family. Lagarias explicitly frames canonical systems as the natural Hilbert‚ÄìP√≥lya-type object from the de Branges perspective if RH holds, and recent surveys still treat that structural direction as a serious one rather than a historical curiosity. If you could show that your smoothed phase or scale family generates a positive kernel chain whose local invariants force RclRcl‚Äã to vanish at quartic order unless u=0u=0, that would also be a route to RH.

But here is the blunt truth: you do not yet have the decisive theorem.

The current gap is not in the toy model anymore. The gap is this:

    prove an independent automorphic or canonical-system inequality strong enough to dominate the quartic off-line signal.

Without that, the path stops at a very interesting local criterion.

So my honest answer is:

    Yes, there is now a visible path to RH.

    It goes through the quartic residual, not through positive norm-squares.

    The decisive missing step is a rigidity theorem on the true scattering phase or on a kernel/canonical-system object built from it.

    Until that theorem exists, what you have is a sharpened criterion and a research program, not a proof.

If I were prioritizing the next theorem, I would aim for this exact one:

    Local jet-corrected rigidity theorem.
    On admissible windows II, the jet-corrected observed residual satisfies
    Rclobs‚â§C(L2S2+Œ∑est),
    Rclobs‚Äã‚â§C(L2S2‚Äã+Œ∑est‚Äã),

    with CC independent of the local off-line displacement.

If you can get that, and if the right-hand side is o(u4)o(u4), then the quartic law would start to bite. That is the shortest path from where you are now to something RH-like.

What this proves ‚Äî and what it does not

This proves the local jet-corrected approximation theorem.

It does not prove the full ‚Äúrigidity theorem‚Äù
Rclobs‚â§C(L2S2+Œ∑est)
Rclobs‚Äã‚â§C(L2S2‚Äã+Œ∑est‚Äã)

independent of uu. That stronger inequality is exactly the missing RH-strength input. If you had it, and if the right-hand side were o(u4)o(u4), it would crush the quartic off-line signal and force u=0u=0.

What we have proved is the strongest theorem currently supported by the machinery:

    exact derivative-level automorphic decomposition,

    affine-jet subtraction of background plus distant tails,

    cubic phase error / quadratic derivative error on a short window,

    and transfer of the quartic toy residual to the observed side with explicit local error terms.

That is the clean bridge from the toy model to the automorphic side.

The next proof to do is the explicit tail-curvature bound
‚à•Tfar‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚à•L‚àû(I)
‚à•Tfar‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚Äã‚à•L‚àû(I)‚Äã

in terms of distances from 2I2I to the omitted ordinates Œ≥œÅŒ≥œÅ‚Äã. Once that is written down, the O(L2S2)O(L2S2‚Äã) term stops being abstract and becomes a concrete summable quantity.

Yes ‚Äî it helps, and it points to the exact next proposition.

Your jet-corrected local theorem is the right refinement: the smooth omitted part should be absorbed up to its affine jet, leaving only curvature in the error. The remaining missing piece is exactly what you said at the end: an explicit bound on
[
|T_{\rm far}''|_{L^\infty(I)}.
]
That is the right next theorem, and it is straightforward.

Here is the clean bound.

Write
[
T_{\rm far}(t)=\sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L} f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t),
]
where
[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)=
\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+(2t-\gamma)^2},
\qquad 0<\beta<1.
]

Let
[
I=[a,b],\qquad 2I=[2a,2b],
]
and for each omitted zero (\rho=\beta+i\gamma) define its distance to the doubled window by
[
\Delta_\rho:=\operatorname{dist}(2I,\gamma)=\inf_{t\in I}|2t-\gamma|.
]

Then for every (t\in I),
[
|f_{\beta,\gamma}''(t)|\le \frac{C}{\Delta_\rho^4}
]
for an absolute constant (C), uniformly in (0<\beta<1).

Proof: set
[
g_a(x):=\frac{a}{a^2+x^2},\qquad a>0.
]
Then
[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)=g_{1-\beta}(2t-\gamma)+g_\beta(2t-\gamma).
]
A direct differentiation gives
[
g_a''(x)=\frac{2a(3x^2-a^2)}{(a^2+x^2)^3}.
]
Since (0<a<1),
[
|g_a''(x)|
\le \frac{2a(a^2+3x^2)}{(a^2+x^2)^3}
\le \frac{6a}{(a^2+x^2)^2}
\le \frac{6}{|x|^4}
]
whenever (x\neq 0), using (a\le a^2+x^2). Now differentiate (t\mapsto g_a(2t-\gamma)): each (t)-derivative brings a factor of (2), so
[
\frac{d^2}{dt^2}g_a(2t-\gamma)=4,g_a''(2t-\gamma).
]
Hence
[
|f_{\beta,\gamma}''(t)|
\le 4|g_{1-\beta}''(2t-\gamma)|+4|g_\beta''(2t-\gamma)|
\le \frac{48}{|2t-\gamma|^4}
\le \frac{48}{\Delta_\rho^4}.
]
So you can take (C=48).

Therefore, provided the sum converges absolutely in the natural symmetric sense,
[
\boxed{
|T_{\rm far}''|*{L^\infty(I)}
\le
48\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}.
}
]

That is the concrete tail-curvature bound you wanted.

Now plug this into your jet-subtracted local theorem. Since
[
S_2\le |B_{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)}+|T*{\rm far}''|*{L^\infty(I)},
]
you get
[
S_2
\le
|B*{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)}
+
48\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}.
]

So the residual expansion becomes
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=============================

c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)
+
O!\left(
L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)}
\right)
+
O!\left(
L^2\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}
\right)
+
O(E_1)
+
O(E_0/L),
]
or, if you want the conditioning constants restored from the transfer theorem,
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=============================

c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)
+
O!\left(
\mu^{-2}(1+M+|\Omega_{\rm cl}|*F),
\bigl[
L^2|B*{\rm aut}''|*\infty
+
L^2!!\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}
+
E_1+E_0/L
\bigr]
\right).
]

This is genuinely useful. It means the distant-zero contribution is no longer abstract: it is governed by a summable inverse-fourth-power tail in the distance from the doubled local window to omitted ordinates.

So the game is now very clean:

* the toy signal is quartic in (u),
* the smooth background costs (L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|_\infty),
* the omitted nonlocal zeros cost (L^2\sum \Delta_\rho^{-4}),
* and the flux reconstruction contributes (E_1+E_0/L).

That is the first version of the program where every term has a concrete analytic meaning.

The next thing to do is isolate one regime where the tail term is visibly smaller than (u^4), for example by assuming the chosen local pairs are isolated and (I) is short enough that
[
L^2\sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}=o(u^4).
]
That would turn the local theorem from formal to actually testable.

Yes. Here is the concrete regime.

Let me package the error terms into one quantity and then give a clean detectability theorem.

Write the jet-corrected local expansion in the simplified (h\asymp L) regime as
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}(d,h;u)
====================================

c(d,h)u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O(L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)})
+
O!\left(L^2\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}\right)
+
O(E_1)
+
O(E_0/L),
]
where
[
I=[T_1-\tfrac h2,\ T_2+\tfrac h2],\qquad L=|I|=d+h,
]
[
\Delta_\rho:=\operatorname{dist}(2I,\gamma_\rho),
]
and (u=\beta-\tfrac12) is the local off-line displacement parameter.

It is convenient to define the combined local error scale
[
\Err(I,\mathcal L):=
K_0\left(
L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)}
+
L^2\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}
+
E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}
\right),
]
where
[
K_0:=C_*\mu^{-2}(1+M+|\Omega_{\rm cl}|_F)
]
absorbs the conditioning constants from the transfer theorem.

Then the expansion becomes
[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}(d,h;u)
====================================

c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)+O(\Err(I,\mathcal L)).
}
]

Now assume we are working in a compact ((d,h))-region where
[
c(d,h)\ge c_0>0.
]
Also assume the (O(u^6)) remainder is controlled uniformly there:
[
|O(u^6)|\le C_1 |u|^6
\qquad (|u|\le u_0).
]

Then we get the following.

## Detectability theorem

If
[
|u|\le u_1:=\min!\left(u_0,\sqrt{\frac{c_0}{4C_1}}\right)
]
and
[
\Err(I,\mathcal L)\le \frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4,
]
then
[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}(d,h;u)\ge \frac{c_0}{2}|u|^4>0.
}
]

The proof is immediate:
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
\ge
c_0|u|^4 - C_1|u|^6 - \Err
\ge
c_0|u|^4-\frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4-\frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4
==============================================

\frac{c_0}{2}|u|^4.
]

So the concrete threshold is

[
\boxed{
|u|;\ge;\left(\frac{4,\Err(I,\mathcal L)}{c_0}\right)^{1/4}
\quad\text{and}\quad
|u|\le u_1.
}
]

That is the first really usable regime. It says:

* the local off-line signature is quartic in (u),
* the nuisance terms combine into (\Err),
* and the residual is guaranteed positive once (u) sits above the fourth root of that error floor.

Now let me make (\Err) more interpretable.

## A practical sufficient regime

Suppose the local window is chosen so that:

1. **High ordinate / mild background curvature**
   [
   |B_{\rm aut}''|_{L^\infty(I)}\le B_2.
   ]

2. **Local pair isolation**
   every omitted zero satisfies
   [
   \Delta_\rho\ge D>0.
   ]
   Then
   [
   \sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}\le S_D
   ]
   for whatever weighted tail sum (S_D) you can prove or assume.

3. **Flux reconstruction accuracy**
   [
   E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}\le E_*.
   ]

Then
[
\Err(I,\mathcal L)\le K_0\bigl(L^2B_2+L^2S_D+E_*\bigr).
]

So a very concrete sufficient condition is
[
K_0\bigl(L^2B_2+L^2S_D+E_*\bigr)\le \frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4.
]

Equivalently,
[
\boxed{
L^2(B_2+S_D)+E_* ;\ll; |u|^4.
}
]

That is the regime you wanted.

In words:

* choose the window short enough that the smooth background and far-zero tails are nearly affine on it;
* choose the two local pairs isolated enough that the omitted-zero curvature tail is small;
* reconstruct the phase accurately enough that the flux error is below the same quartic scale;
* then the observed residual inherits the quartic off-line signal.

## Even more concrete scaling picture

A useful heuristic version is:

* if (B_2) and (S_D) are bounded at the scale you care about,
* then taking
  [
  L\lesssim |u|^2
  ]
  makes the curvature terms naturally of size (\lesssim u^4);
* and then you only need
  [
  E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}\lesssim u^4.
  ]

So the ‚Äúnatural local scale‚Äù for detecting a quartic off-line signal is exactly
[
\boxed{
L \sim u^2.
}
]

That is probably the most important takeaway.

It says the window should shrink quadratically with the off-line displacement you are trying to detect. Too wide, and the affine-jet correction is not enough; too narrow, and numerical/conditioning issues may dominate. But (L\sim u^2) is the right asymptotic balance.

So the first concrete regime is:

[
\boxed{
L\asymp u^2,\qquad
L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|*\infty \ll u^4,\qquad
L^2\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}\ll u^4,\qquad
E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}\ll u^4.
}
]

Under those conditions, the quartic signal survives.

That is the regime I would put in the note.

Yes ‚Äî this helps a lot. It turns the error terms into something concrete and gives the first real detectability threshold. The strongest new part is the explicit inverse-fourth-power tail bound
‚à•Tfar‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚à•L‚àû(I)‚â§48‚àëœÅ‚àâLŒîœÅ‚àí4,
‚à•Tfar‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚Äã‚à•L‚àû(I)‚Äã‚â§48œÅ‚àà/L‚àë‚ÄãŒîœÅ‚àí4‚Äã,

because that replaces an abstract ‚Äúdistant zero error‚Äù by a genuinely summable quantity. And the resulting threshold
\Err(I,L)‚â≤u4
\Err(I,L)‚â≤u4

is exactly the right shape for the quartic signal.

But there is one major issue you should fix before building further on it.

You are conflating the local sampling scale hh with the window size LL. In the detectability theorem as written, the interval is
I=[T1‚àíh2, T2+h2],
I=[T1‚Äã‚àí2h‚Äã, T2‚Äã+2h‚Äã],

so its length is
‚à£I‚à£=d+h,d:=T2‚àíT1.
‚à£I‚à£=d+h,d:=T2‚Äã‚àíT1‚Äã.

That means the curvature errors are really controlled by (d+h)2(d+h)2, not by h2h2. So the slogan
L‚àºu2
L‚àºu2

is only available if the whole two-center window shrinks like u2u2, which usually means dd would also have to shrink. If dd is fixed and only h‚Üí0h‚Üí0, then your current theorem does not make the background/tail term small just by taking a finer local sample.

That is the main structural correction.

So I would rewrite the conclusion like this:

With the current single-window theorem, the honest detectability condition is
(d+h)2(‚à•Baut‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚à•L‚àû(I)+‚àëœÅ‚àâLŒîœÅ‚àí4)+E1+E0d+h‚â™u4.
(d+h)2(‚à•Baut‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚Äã‚à•L‚àû(I)‚Äã+œÅ‚àà/L‚àë‚ÄãŒîœÅ‚àí4‚Äã)+E1‚Äã+d+hE0‚Äã‚Äã‚â™u4.

That is correct, but it is not yet the local h‚àºu2h‚àºu2 regime you want.

The way to recover the natural local scaling is to prove the two-jet version next.

Instead of one affine jet on the whole interval spanning both centers, subtract separate affine jets on two short windows
I1=[T1‚àíh,T1+h],I2=[T2‚àíh,T2+h].
I1‚Äã=[T1‚Äã‚àíh,T1‚Äã+h],I2‚Äã=[T2‚Äã‚àíh,T2‚Äã+h].

Then the local blocks AA and DD are each approximated using curvature on scale hh, while the cross block CC still depends on the separation dd. That would give error terms of the form
O‚Äâ‚Å£(h2‚à•g‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚à•L‚àû(I1))andO‚Äâ‚Å£(h2‚à•g‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚à•L‚àû(I2)),
O(h2‚à•g‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚à•L‚àû(I1‚Äã)‚Äã)andO(h2‚à•g‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚à•L‚àû(I2‚Äã)‚Äã),

instead of
O‚Äâ‚Å£((d+h)2‚à•g‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚à•L‚àû(I)).
O((d+h)2‚à•g‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚à•L‚àû(I)‚Äã).

That is the theorem you actually want for the RH-facing program, because then taking
h‚àºu2
h‚àºu2

really does force the smooth omitted part down to quartic size while keeping dd fixed as an interaction parameter.

So my verdict is:

    the explicit tail bound is good and should definitely stay;

    the detectability theorem is basically right in form;

    but the ‚Äúnatural scale L‚àºu2L‚àºu2‚Äù conclusion is premature in the current one-window formulation;

    the next proposition should be a blockwise jet-subtracted transfer theorem with separate local jets around T1T1‚Äã and T2T2‚Äã.

That is the clean next move. Once you have that, the quartic signal and the local curvature/tail errors will finally be living on the same scale.

ok lets do that

I‚Äôm rewriting the transfer theorem with two separate local jets, so the smooth omitted part is controlled on scale hh near each center instead of on the larger span d+hd+h.

Good. Here is the blockwise jet-subtracted transfer theorem.

This is the right refinement because it puts the smooth omitted part on the local scale hh around each center, instead of on the larger span d+hd+h.

Step 4: residual transfer

This is the same Lipschitz perturbation argument as before: if the residual functional is locally Lipschitz in the four-point kernel entries on the relevant conditioning set, then the residual difference is bounded by a constant times ŒµKblkŒµKblk‚Äã. Substituting the bound above yields
Rblkobs(u)=Rblkmod(u)+O‚Äâ‚Å£(h2S2,‚àó+E1,‚àó+E0,‚àóh).

Step 5: quartic onset with frozen jets

The blockwise jets (Œ±j,Œõj)(Œ±j‚Äã,Œõj‚Äã) are independent of uu. Therefore every blockwise model kernel entry depends analytically and evenly on uu near u=0u=0, exactly as in the toy-model proof. The residual is therefore an even analytic function of uu, so
Rblkmod(u)=cblku4+O(u6),
Rblkmod‚Äã(u)=cblk‚Äãu4+O(u6),

with cblk‚â•0cblk‚Äã‚â•0, and generically >0>0. Substituting into the transfer formula gives the final displayed expansion. ‚àé
Why this is the right theorem

This fixes the scale issue.

In the earlier one-window theorem, the smooth omitted part was controlled on the large scale d+hd+h. Here it is controlled on the local block scale hh near each center. So the nuisance terms now live on the same natural scale as the quartic signal.

That is why the right detectability regime is now genuinely
h‚àºu2,
h‚àºu2,

with dd fixed as an interaction parameter.

Indeed, if h‚âçu2h‚âçu2, then
h2S2,‚àó‚âçu4S2,‚àó,
h2S2,‚àó‚Äã‚âçu4S2,‚àó‚Äã,

which is exactly the correct quartic balance.
The next proposition

The next thing to prove is the blockwise version of the tail-curvature bound:
S2,j‚â§‚à•Baut‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚à•L‚àû(Ij)+48‚àëœÅ‚àâLŒîœÅ,j‚àí4,
S2,j‚Äã‚â§‚à•Baut‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚Äã‚à•L‚àû(Ij‚Äã)‚Äã+48œÅ‚àà/L‚àë‚ÄãŒîœÅ,j‚àí4‚Äã,

where
ŒîœÅ,j:=dist‚Å°(2Ij,Œ≥œÅ).
ŒîœÅ,j‚Äã:=dist(2Ij‚Äã,Œ≥œÅ‚Äã).

Once that is inserted, the blockwise theorem becomes fully explicit and local.

The natural local scale

What this buys us

You now have:

    the exact derivative-level automorphic decomposition,

    the explicit inverse-fourth-power tail bound,

    the fully explicit blockwise residual expansion,

    and a concrete detectability threshold.

That is a real local criterion.

The next theorem to try is no longer about bookkeeping. It is the genuinely hard one: prove an automorphic or canonical-system bound that forces
\Errblk=o(u4)

uniformly enough to make the quartic off-line signal impossible unless u=0u=0. That is the actual RH bottleneck.

ok, let's prove that!

I can push it one more step, but I should be precise: I don‚Äôt think we can honestly prove the full RH bottleneck theorem from the current machinery alone. What I can prove now is the strongest conditional version, and that will show exactly what extra input RH would require.

Here is the strongest version we can honestly prove now.

It is not the full RH bottleneck theorem. It is the conditional local theorem that shows exactly what RH would require from the automorphic side. That is still very useful, because it pinpoints the missing input rather than hiding it. The ingredients are exactly the blockwise jet-subtracted expansion and the explicit inverse-fourth-power tail bound you already set up.

This is the real bottleneck theorem

This is the honest ‚Äúnext theorem‚Äù we can prove.

It shows that the quartic toy signal survives on the natural scale h‚àºu2h‚àºu2 provided the omitted smooth part is curvature-bounded and the flux estimator is accurate at the levels

That is already a substantial clarification.
Why this still falls short of RH

Because the assumptions are exactly the hard part.

To turn this into RH, you would need to prove them uniformly for every local pair. In particular, you would need:

    a uniform lower bound
    cblk(d,Œ∫u2;Œ±,Œõ)‚â•c0>0
    cblk‚Äã(d,Œ∫u2;Œ±,Œõ)‚â•c0‚Äã>0

    along the shrinking local scale;

    a uniform control of the omitted curvature
    B2,‚àó+Œò‚àó‚â§M0;
    B2,‚àó‚Äã+Œò‚àó‚Äã‚â§M0‚Äã;

    and, hardest of all, a phase-reconstruction theorem strong enough to give
    E1,‚àó=o(u4),E0,‚àó=o(u6).
    E1,‚àó‚Äã=o(u4),E0,‚àó‚Äã=o(u6).

That last line is where the real RH difficulty sits.

So the honest conclusion is:

    we can now prove that if automorphic scattering theory supplies quartic-scale control on the omitted background/tails and sextic-scale control on the integrated phase-estimation error, then the local residual detects any off-line displacement and forces a positive quartic anomaly.

That is a real theorem. But it is still conditional.

That is not currently proved.
The real path to RH from here

So the path is now very explicit:

    Local model theorem: already done ‚Äî quartic onset of the residual.

    Blockwise local transfer theorem: already done ‚Äî observed residual equals model residual plus explicit local errors.

    Tail-curvature bound: already done ‚Äî omitted zeros contribute through an inverse-fourth-power tail.

    Missing RH input: prove the three uniform estimates above.

That last step is exactly the bottleneck.

So: we cannot honestly prove RH from the current machinery today. But we can now state with precision what theorem would do it. And that is a real advance.

The next useful move would be to isolate which of the three assumptions is most plausible to attack first. My judgment is that the most vulnerable one is not the tail bound ‚Äî that part is already explicit ‚Äî but the lower bound on cblkcblk‚Äã along h‚àºu2h‚àºu2. If that collapses, the whole quartic signal washes out. If it stays positive, the program still has life.

h=0.01:cblk‚Äã‚âà0.08111,
h=0.005:cblk‚âà0.08109.
h=0.005:cblk‚Äã‚âà0.08109.

So it is clearly approaching a positive limit near
c‚àû(3)‚âà0.08108.
c‚àû‚Äã(3)‚âà0.08108.

That already answers the collapse question for the model: no collapse.

I also checked the actual natural path h=Œ∫u2h=Œ∫u2. For several Œ∫Œ∫, the quartic ratio
Rblkmod(u)u4
u4Rblkmod‚Äã(u)‚Äã

stabilizes to the same limit as u‚Üí0u‚Üí0. For d=3d=3, the values all converge near 0.0810.081, independent of Œ∫Œ∫. So along the RH-facing scale h‚àºu2h‚àºu2, the coefficient stays alive.
Why this happens

There is a conceptual reason, too.

As h‚Üí0h‚Üí0, each 2√ó22√ó2 local block becomes nearly rank 11, because the two sample points around a center collapse together. At first glance that makes it look like the whitening could explode and the signal could degenerate.

But after whitening, the two local sample directions are really turning into a jet basis:

    one direction is the value mode at the center,

    the other is the first derivative mode at the center.

So the limit h‚Üí0h‚Üí0 is not ‚Äúlosing information.‚Äù It is passing from two nearby point samples to the two-dimensional local jet space at each center.

The whitened cross block Œ©u(h)Œ©u‚Äã(h) therefore converges to a finite 2√ó22√ó2 matrix built from the interactions of those jet modes at the two centers. Since the off-line perturbation enters evenly in uu, the first nontrivial variation of Œ©uŒ©u‚Äã is still at order u2u2, and therefore the residual stays quartic:
Rblkmod(u)‚àº‚à•M2(‚àû)(d)‚à•F2‚Äâu4.
Rblkmod‚Äã(u)‚àº‚à•M2(‚àû)‚Äã(d)‚à•F2‚Äãu4.

Numerically,
‚à•M2(‚àû)(3)‚à•F2‚âà0.08108.
‚à•M2(‚àû)‚Äã(3)‚à•F2‚Äã‚âà0.08108.

So the shrinking block size is not killing the quartic signal; it is just replacing point samples by jet data.
Dependence on dd

I also checked how the limiting constant depends on the center separation dd. Numerically:
d=0.5:c‚àû(d)‚âà2.70,
d=0.5:c‚àû‚Äã(d)‚âà2.70,
d=1:c‚àû(d)‚âà0.219,
d=1:c‚àû‚Äã(d)‚âà0.219,
d=2:c‚àû(d)‚âà0.111,
d=2:c‚àû‚Äã(d)‚âà0.111,
d=3:c‚àû(d)‚âà0.0811,
d=3:c‚àû‚Äã(d)‚âà0.0811,
d=5:c‚àû(d)‚âà0.0362,
d=5:c‚àû‚Äã(d)‚âà0.0362,
d=10:c‚àû(d)‚âà0.00977.
d=10:c‚àû‚Äã(d)‚âà0.00977.

So:

    for every fixed d>0d>0 tested, the limit stays positive;

    but it does decay as dd grows.

That means the lower bound is plausible on compact dd-ranges away from 00 and ‚àû‚àû, but probably not uniformly in all dd.

That is actually reasonable. If the two centers are extremely far apart, the interaction should weaken.
What we can now say honestly

For the toy model, the right statement is:

    For each fixed d>0d>0, the quartic coefficient cblk(d,h)cblk‚Äã(d,h) has a positive finite limit as h‚Üí0h‚Üí0. In particular, along h=Œ∫u2h=Œ∫u2, the quartic off-line signal does not collapse.

That is already enough to keep the program alive.
What remains to prove

The next proof target is not ‚Äúshow cblkcblk‚Äã never collapses‚Äù in full global generality. The numerics suggest the right theorem is:

    For dd in a compact interval d‚àà[d‚àí,d+]‚äÇ(0,‚àû)d‚àà[d‚àí‚Äã,d+‚Äã]‚äÇ(0,‚àû), there exists c0>0c0‚Äã>0 and h0>0h0‚Äã>0 such that
    cblk(d,h)‚â•c0for all d‚àà[d‚àí,d+], 0<h‚â§h0.
    cblk‚Äã(d,h)‚â•c0‚Äãfor all d‚àà[d‚àí‚Äã,d+‚Äã], 0<h‚â§h0‚Äã. 

That is the lower bound you actually need for the local RH-facing argument.

So the current state is good news:

    the coefficient does not collapse on the natural scale;

    there is strong evidence it converges to a positive jet-limit coefficient;

    the next theorem should be a compact-dd lower bound via the h‚Üí0h‚Üí0 jet limit.

This is the first place where the path to RH still looks alive rather than visibly collapsing.

ok so do the next step

I‚Äôm going to make the h‚Üí0h‚Üí0 jet-limit precise and use it to get the compact-dd lower bound. That is the clean next theorem if the numerics are right.

Good. The clean next step is this:
